                if ("connection" in navigator) {
                    if (navigator.connection.saveData != true) {
                        var preloadLink = document.createElement("link");
                        preloadLink.rel = "preload";

                        preloadLink.type = "font/woff2";
                        preloadLink.crossOrigin = "";
                        document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
                    }
                }
            }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41586"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome sequence of the progenitor of the wheat D genome Aegilops tauschii"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature 2017 551:7681"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2017-11-01"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2017 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="A combination of advanced sequencing and mapping techniques is used to produce a reference genome of Aegilops tauschii, progenitor of the wheat D genome, providing a valuable resource for comparative genetic studies. Sequencing the genomes of crops plants provides useful resources for crop improvement and breeding. Jan Dvo&#345;&#225;k, Katrien Devos, Steven Salzberg and colleagues report a reference genome for Aegilops tauschii, the diploid progenitor of the D genome of hexaploid wheat. They use a combination of ordered-clone genome sequencing, whole-genome shotgun sequencing and BioNano optical genome mapping to assemble this large and highly repetitive genome. This provides a useful resource for comparative genomics studies of wheat. Aegilops tauschii is the diploid progenitor of the D genome of hexaploid wheat1 (Triticum aestivum, genomes AABBDD) and an important genetic resource for wheat2,3,4. The large size and highly repetitive nature of the Ae. tauschii genome has until now precluded the development of a reference-quality genome sequence5. Here we use an array of advanced technologies, including ordered-clone genome sequencing, whole-genome shotgun sequencing, and BioNano optical genome mapping, to generate a reference-quality genome sequence for Ae. tauschii ssp. strangulata accession AL8/78, which is closely related to the wheat D genome. We show that compared to other sequenced plant genomes, including a much larger conifer genome, the Ae. tauschii genome contains unprecedented amounts of very similar repeated sequences. Our genome comparisons reveal that the Ae. tauschii genome has a greater number of dispersed duplicated genes than other sequenced genomes and its chromosomes have been structurally evolving an order of magnitude faster than those of other grass genomes. The decay of colinearity with other grass genomes correlates with recombination rates along chromosomes. We propose that the vast amounts of very similar repeated sequences cause frequent errors in recombination and lead to gene duplications and structural chromosome changes that drive fast genome evolution."/>

itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" Mayer</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff7">7</a>,<a href="#Aff10">10</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Plant Genome and Systems Biology, Helmholtz Zentrum München" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.4567.0, 0000 0004 0483 2525, Plant Genome and Systems Biology, Helmholtz Zentrum München, Neuherberg, Germany" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="School of Life Sciences Weihenstephan, Technical University of Munich" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.6936.a, 0000000123222966, School of Life Sciences Weihenstephan, Technical University of Munich, Munich, Germany" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Fu_Hao-Lu" data-author-popup="auth-Fu_Hao-Lu">Fu-Hao Lu</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff11">11</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="John Innes Centre, Norwich Research Park" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.14830.3e, 0000 0001 2175 7246, Department Cell and Developmental Biology, John Innes Centre, Norwich Research Park, Norwich, UK" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Michael_W_-Bevan" data-author-popup="auth-Michael_W_-Bevan">Michael W.             <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs3" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs3-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs3">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs3-content"><p><i>Aegilops tauschii</i> is the diploid progenitor of the D genome of hexaploid wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="McFadden, E. S. &amp; Sears, E. R. The origin of Triticum spelta and its free-threshing hexaploid relatives. J. Hered. 37, 81–89, 107 (1946)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1097">1</a></sup> (<i>Triticum aestivum</i>, genomes AABBDD) and an important genetic resource for wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Gill, B. S. et al. Resistance in Aegilops squarrosa to wheat leaf rust, wheat powdery mildew, greenbug, and Hessian fly. Plant Dis. 70, 553–556 (1986)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1104">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Ogbonnaya, F. C. et al. Synthetic hexaploids: harnessing species of the primary gene pool for wheat improvement. Plant Breed. Rev. 37, 35–122 (2013)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1107">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Periyannan, S. et al. The gene Sr33, an ortholog of barley Mla genes, encodes resistance to wheat stem rust race Ug99. Science 341, 786–788 (2013)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1110">4</a></sup>. The large size and highly repetitive nature of the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome has until now precluded the development of a reference-quality genome sequence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Jia, J. et al. Aegilops tauschii draft genome sequence reveals a gene repertoire for wheat adaptation. Nature 496, 91–95 (2013)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1117">5</a></sup>. Here we use an array of advanced technologies, including ordered-clone genome sequencing, whole-genome shotgun sequencing, and BioNano optical genome mapping, to generate a reference-quality genome sequence for <i>Ae. tauschii</i> ssp. <i>strangulata</i> accession AL8/78, which is closely related to the wheat D genome. We show that compared to other sequenced plant genomes, including a much larger conifer genome, the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome contains unprecedented amounts of very similar repeated sequences. Our genome comparisons reveal that the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome has a greater number of dispersed duplicated genes than other sequenced genomes and its chromosomes have been structurally evolving an order of magnitude faster than those of other grass genomes. The decay of colinearity with other grass genomes correlates with recombination rates along chromosomes. We propose that the vast amounts of very similar repeated sequences cause frequent errors in recombination and lead to gene duplications and structural chromosome changes that drive fast genome evolution.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The <i>Ae. tauschii</i> AL8/78 genome sequence was assembled in five steps (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 1a</a>). The core was assembly Aet v1.1 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 1b</a>) based on sequences of 42,822 bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC) clones. This assembly was merged with a whole-genome shotgun (WGS) assembly (Aet WGS 1.0) and WGS Pacific Biosciences mega-reads<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Zimin, A. V. et al. Hybrid assembly of the large and highly repetitive genome of Aegilops tauschii, a progenitor of bread wheat, with the MaSuRCA mega-reads algorithm. Genome Res. 27, 787–792 (2017)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1175">6</a></sup> to extend scaffolds and close gaps, thereby producing assembly Aet v2.0 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 1b, c</a>). Misassembled scaffolds were detected with the aid of an AL8/78 optical BioNano genome (BNG) map and resolved, producing assembly Aet v3.0 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 1d, e</a>). Two additional BNG maps were constructed and, along with the genetic and physical maps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Luo, M. C. et al. A 4-gigabase physical map unlocks the structure and evolution of the complex genome of Aegilops tauschii, the wheat D-genome progenitor. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 110, 7940–7945 (2013)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1186">7</a></sup>, used in super-scaffolding and building pseudomolecules for the final assembly, Aet v4.0 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 1d, f, g</a>).</p><p>The combined length of the pseudomolecules was 4,025,304,143 bp, and they contained 95.2% of the sequence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig5">Extended Data Fig. 2a</a>). About 200 Mb of the super-scaffolds remained unassigned and 76 Mb of the AL8/78 BNG contigs were devoid of aligned super-scaffolds. We conclude therefore that the size of the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome is about 4.3 Gb.</p><p>To assess the accuracy of our assembly, sequences of 195 independently sequenced and assembled AL8/78 BAC clones<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Massa, A. N. et al. Gene space dynamics during the evolution of Aegilops tauschii, Brachypodium distachyon, Oryza sativa, and Sorghum bicolor genomes. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 2537–2547 (2011)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1205">8</a></sup>, which contained 25,540,177 bp in 2,405 unordered contigs, were aligned to Aet v3.0. Five contigs failed to align and six extended partly into gaps, accounting for 0.25% of the total length of the contigs. Seven BAC contigs, although aligned, contained internal structural differences. The remaining contigs aligned end-to-end with an average identity of 99.75%. Considering the likely possibility that errors existed in both assemblies, this validation indicated that our assembly is remarkably accurate.</p><p>More work is nevertheless needed to order super-scaffolds in the pericentromeric regions of the pseudomolecules (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig5">Extended Data Fig. 2a</a>). The ends of the pseudomolecules also need attention. Arrays of the rice (<i>Oryza sativa</i>) telomeric repeat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Mizuno, H. et al. Chromosome-specific distribution of nucleotide substitutions in telomeric repeats of rice (Oryza sativa L.). Mol. Biol. Evol. 25, 62–68 (2008)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1218">9</a></sup> (TTTAGGG) were detected in all pseudomolecules and in three of the unassigned scaffolds, but in only four pseudomolecules was such an array terminally located (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig5">Extended Data Fig. 2a</a>) and presumably marked a telomere.</p><p>The wheat and <i>Aegilops</i> genomes have seven chromosomes, which evolved by dysploid reductions from twelve ancestral chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Gale, M. D. &amp; Devos, K. M. Plant comparative genetics after 10 years. Science 282, 656–659 (1998)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1232">10</a></sup>. The dominant form of dysploid reduction in the grass family is the nested chromosome insertion (NCI), in which a chromosome is inserted, usually by its termini, into the centromere-adjacent region of another chromosome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Luo, M. C. et al. Genome comparisons reveal a dominant mechanism of chromosome number reduction in grasses and accelerated genome evolution in Triticeae. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 15780–15785 (2009)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1236">11</a></sup>. <i>Ae. tauschii</i> chromosomes 1D, 2D, 4D, and 7D originated by NCIs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig5">Extended Data Fig. 2b</a>). Chromosome 5D probably originated by an end-to-end fusion of the short arms of ancestral chromosomes corresponding to rice chromosomes Os9 and Os12, followed by a reciprocal translocation involving the Os9 section of 5DL and the Os3 section of 4DS (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig5">Extended Data Fig. 2b–d</a>).</p><p>A nearly perfect array of telomeric repeats 141-bp long was located on <i>Ae. tauschii</i> chromosome 2D at 33,306,010 to 33,306,151 bp, which is within a 2D NCI site (31,408,197 to 34,183,608 bp, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#MOESM1">Supplementary Data 1</a>), indicating that telomeric repeats may be directly involved in the NCI process. An NCI may be a special case of telomere-driven ectopic recombination. Of the 23 ancestral chromosome termini that we could study by investigating the colinearity of <i>Ae. tauschii</i> pseudomolecules with those of rice, 14 (61%) have been involved in one or more inversions or end-to-end fusions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig5">Extended Data Fig. 2b</a>).</p><p>Transposable elements (TEs) represented 84.4% of the genome sequence. By far the most abundant (65.9% of the sequence) were the long terminal repeat retrotransposons (LTR-RTs) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig6">Extended Data Fig. 3a</a>). <i>Gypsy</i> and <i>CACTA</i> were the most abundant RNA and DNA transposon super-families, respectively. Most of the 1,113 new TE families discovered were low in copy number, from one to three complete elements; new short interspersed nuclear element (more commonly known as SINE) families were exceptional in this respect (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig6">Extended Data Fig. 3b</a>).</p><p>The density of a pseudomolecule of the <i>Gypsy</i> super-family and unclassified LTR-RTs increased from the telomere towards the centromere whereas the density of the <i>Copia</i> and <i>CACTA</i> super-families mirrored exon density and increased in the opposite direction (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>). Individual families often deviated from these general patterns, as indicated by the mean and median distances to the centromere of the 22 most abundant LTR-RT families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig6">Extended Data Fig. 3c</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Aegilops tauschii TEs."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: <i>Aegilops tauschii</i> TEs.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature24486/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature24486/MediaObjects/41586_2017_BFnature24486_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature24486/MediaObjects/41586_2017_BFnature24486_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="592" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Heat maps of densities along the pseudomolecules of centromeric LTR-RT <i>cereba</i>, exons, <i>Copia</i> and <i>Gypsy</i> LTR-RT super-families, unclassified LTR-RTs, other types of class I (RNA) TEs, <i>CACTA</i> (DNA) TEs, and other types of class II (DNA) transposons. Red arrowheads or brackets indicate the positions of the centromeres. Green arrowheads indicate the sites of NCIs (1D, 2D, 4D, and 7D) and chromosome (telomere) fusion (5D). <b>b</b>, Normalized insertion (intensity) rates of the 22 most abundant <i>Copia</i> (red) and <i>Gypsy</i> (blue) families in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome during the past 3 Myr. <b>c</b>, The <i>k</i>-mer uniqueness ratio for <i>Ae. tauschii</i> (Aet), compared to <i>P. taeda</i> (Pt), <i>O. sativa</i> (Os), <i>B. distachyon</i> (Bd), <i>Setaria italica</i> (Si), <i>S. bicolor</i> (Sb), and <i>Homo sapiens</i> (Hs). <b>d</b>, <b>e</b>, Ages of TEs in the 11 distally located families (<b>d</b>) and the 11 proximally located families (<b>e</b>) from the 22 families in <b>b</b>. The age difference between the two groups is statistically significant (<i>P</i> = 0.003, two-sided <i>t</i>-test, <i>n</i> = 22).</p><p>
<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#MOESM6">PowerPoint slide</a>
</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature24486/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p><i>Gypsy</i> family 12, which is homologous to the barley (<i>Hordeum vulgare</i>) centromeric retrotransposon <i>cereba</i> located at barley centromere cores<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Hudakova, S. et al. Sequence organization of barley centromeres. Nucleic Acids Res. 29, 5029–5035 (2001)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1420">12</a></sup>, clustered in the centromeric regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>). We found 311 complete elements and 79,515 truncated elements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig6">Extended Data Fig. 3d</a>). The unassigned scaffolds contained more complete elements compared to truncated elements than the pseudomolecules, which suggests that unassigned scaffolds are enriched for centromere core sequences.</p><p>Our dating of LTR-RT insertions suggested a TE amplification peak about 1 million years ago (Ma). Accepting this result at its face value neglects amplification dynamics in individual families<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Baucom, R. S. et al. Exceptional diversity, non-random distribution, and rapid evolution of retroelements in the B73 maize genome. PLoS Genet. 5, e1000732 (2009)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1433">13</a></sup> and TE removal by deletions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Devos, K. M., Brown, J. K. M. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Genome size reduction through illegitimate recombination counteracts genome expansion in Arabidopsis. Genome Res. 12, 1075–1079 (2002)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1437">14</a></sup>. Modelling the demography of LTR-RTs on the basis of the rates of TE ‘births’ and ‘deaths’ in individual families showed that LTR-RT families were subjected to sequential bursts of amplification followed by silencing over the past 3 million years (Myr) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>). TEs older than 3 Myr were mostly absent, which is consistent with fast turnover of intergenic DNA in Triticeae genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Dubcovsky, J. &amp; Dvorak, J. Genome plasticity a key factor in the success of polyploid wheat under domestication. Science 316, 1862–1866 (2007)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1444">15</a></sup>.</p><p>Other evidence for the very high rate of replacement of TEs in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome was provided by <i>Ae. tauschii k</i>-mer uniqueness ratio analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>). The ratio is the percentage of the genome that is covered by unique sequences of length <i>k</i> or longer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Schatz, M. C., Delcher, A. L. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Assembly of large genomes using second-generation sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 1165–1173 (2010)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1464">16</a></sup>. The <i>Ae. tauschii</i> ratio was the lowest among the seven genomes compared in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>, including the much larger pine (<i>Pinus taeda</i>) genome. Not only is the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome exceptionally repetitive, but also its TEs are highly similar to each other.</p><p>LTR-RT families in the proximal chromosome regions were older on average than families in the distal chromosome regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig1">Fig. 1d, e</a>). This is most likely caused by faster deletion of DNA from distal chromosome regions than from proximal chromosome regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Baucom, R. S. et al. Exceptional diversity, non-random distribution, and rapid evolution of retroelements in the B73 maize genome. PLoS Genet. 5, e1000732 (2009)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1487">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Dvorak, J., Yang, Z.-L., You, F. M. &amp; Luo, M. C. Deletion polymorphism in wheat chromosome regions with contrasting recombination rates. Genetics 168, 1665–1675 (2004)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1490">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1493">18</a></sup>.</p><p>Our annotation pipeline (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig7">Extended Data Fig. 4a, b</a>) annotated 83,117 genes in Aet v4.0 and allocated 39,622 of them into the high-confidence class (HCC) (gene set v2.0) and the remaining 43,495 into the low-confidence class (LCC) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 5a</a>). Of the HCC genes, 38,775 were in the pseudomolecules and 847 (2.2%) were in unassigned scaffolds. The total length of predicted HCC genes was 316,517,346 bp (7.5%) and the total length of their mRNAs was 145,062,217 bp (3.4%). Gene annotation was validated by a search for 1,440 BUSCO genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Simão, F. A. et al. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics (2015)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1506">19</a></sup>, of which 1,408 (97.8%) were correctly predicted among the 83,117 genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 5b</a>). <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genes were compared with genes annotated in four grass genomes and the <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 5c</a>). <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genes were the longest, had the longest mean exon length, and together with barley genes had the longest transcript lengths among the genomes. Otherwise, they were similar to genes in the other genomes, except for having a lower average number of exons.</p><p>The <i>Ae. tauschii</i> HCC genes and HCC genes annotated in the barley, <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i>, rice, and sorghum (<i>Sorghum bicolor</i>) genomes were clustered into gene families and compared (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>). The genomes contained between 422 and 1,320 genome-unique clusters and shared 12,607 clusters, probably representative of the gene core of grass genomes. The <i>Ae. tauschii</i> and barley HCC gene sets exclusively shared 1,666 clusters, likely to be representative of gene families unique to Triticeae.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Ae. tauschii genes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature24486/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature24486/MediaObjects/41586_2017_BFnature24486_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature24486/MediaObjects/41586_2017_BFnature24486_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="217" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, OrthoMCL gene family clustering of the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> HCC genes with those of <i>S. bicolor</i>, <i>B. distachyon</i>, <i>O. sativa</i>, and <i>H. vulgare</i>. The first number below each species name is the total number of genes analysed, the second number is the number of genes in clusters, and the third number is the number of gene clusters. Numbers in the sections of the diagram indicate the numbers of clusters (gene groups), not individual genes. <b>b</b>, OrthoMCL clustering of <i>Ae. tauschii</i> HCC genes with hexaploid wheat cv. Chinese Spring (CS) genes classified by A-, B-, and D-genome origin or unclassified origin (U). <b>c</b>, Distribution of RGA multi-gene loci (filled ovals) along the seven <i>Ae. tauschii</i> pseudomolecules including coiled-coil (CC)–NBS–leucine-rich repeats (LRRs) (red ovals), receptor-like kinases (black ovals), receptor-like proteins (yellow ovals), NBSs (blue ovals), and NBS–LRRs (green ovals). Centromeric regions are indicated by the thicker horizontal line for each pseudomolecule. A locus was considered to be multi-gene if it contained at least three genes of a specific RGA class with a maximum distance between genes of 300 kb.</p><p>
<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#MOESM7">PowerPoint slide</a>
</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature24486/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>A similar comparison was made with genes annotated in hexaploid wheat cv. Chinese Spring<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Clavijo, B. J. et al. An improved assembly and annotation of the allohexaploid wheat genome identifies complete families of agronomic genes and provides genomic evidence for chromosomal translocations. Genome Res. 27, 885–896 (2017)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1607">20</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). The numbers of genome-unique clusters were lower in the wheat genomes than in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome, which may reflect the phylogenetic proximity of the genomes and the correlation between the number of genome-unique clusters and the numbers of genes annotated in the genome (<i>r</i> = 0.85 and 0.98, see Methods). The <i>Ae. tauschii</i> and wheat genomes shared 15,180 clusters, probably representative of the gene core of the wheat and <i>Aegilops</i> genomes. The wheat B genome shared more clusters exclusively with the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome and with the wheat D genome than did the A genome.</p><p>Several BLAST or BLAT search approaches were employed to estimate the numbers of gene differences between the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> AL8/78 genome and Chinese Spring wheat D genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 5d, e</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#MOESM1">Supplementary Data 2</a>). If the orthologue for an <i>Ae. tauschii</i> gene is absent from the wheat D genome, the best search hit will most likely be a paralogue in any of the three wheat genomes or an orthologue in the A or B genomes. Large percentages of such hits were observed (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 5d, e</a>). They exceeded the 0.17 and 0.27% of genes previously estimated to have been deleted from the wheat D genome since the origin of hexaploid wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Dvorak, J., Yang, Z.-L., You, F. M. &amp; Luo, M. C. Deletion polymorphism in wheat chromosome regions with contrasting recombination rates. Genetics 168, 1665–1675 (2004)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1649">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Xie, J. et al. Sequencing and comparative analyses of Aegilops tauschii chromosome arm 3DS reveal rapid evolution of Triticeae genomes. J. Genet. Genomics 44, 51–61 (2017)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1652">21</a></sup> by nearly two orders of magnitude. Bidirectional BLAST searches showed additional reductions in successful searches, indicating the absence of orthologues in both the wheat D genome and the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome and differences in gene annotation in these two genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 5d</a>). Only 87.4% of <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genes were correctly located by BLATN searches in the D genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 5e</a>), further highlighting the magnitude of polymorphism for gene presence and absence (copy number variation) in these genomes.</p><p>Some of the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genes and gene clusters classified as unique or Triticeae specific may be of practical importance. Prolamin genes, which represent several seed-storage protein families unique to Triticeae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Dong, L. et al. Rapid evolutionary dynamics in a 2.8-Mb chromosomal region containing multiple prolamin and resistance gene families in Aegilops tauschii. Plant J. 87, 495–506 (2016)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1675">22</a></sup>, are central to the bread-making properties of wheat flour. We discovered and characterized 31 prolamin genes in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome sequence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 5g</a>).</p><p>Another class of <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genes that are of practical importance are disease resistance genes. Using a disease resistance gene analogue (RGA) prediction pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Li, P. C. et al. RGAugury: a pipeline for genome-wide prediction of resistance gene analogs (RGAs) in plants. BMC Genomics 17, 852 (2016)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1691">23</a></sup>, we annotated 1,762 RGAs in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome sequence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig9">Extended Data Fig. 6a</a>) and list them in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#MOESM1">Supplementary Data 3</a>. Nucleotide-binding site (NBS)-type RGAs tend to cluster near the ends of chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig9">Extended Data Fig. 6b</a>). That is true for RGA multi-gene loci in general. They show a distinct preference for distal chromosome regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>). A total of 81 RGA multi-gene loci were identified (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig9">Extended Data Fig. 6c</a>). The largest number was in 6D (20 loci), followed by 7D and 2D (16 and 15 loci, respectively), and the smallest number was in 4D (1 locus).</p><p>Of the 38,775 HCC genes located on the pseudomolecules, only 5,050 (13.0%) were single-copy genes. By far the most abundant were dispersed duplicated genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 5f</a>). These were disproportionally more abundant in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome than in the genomes of <i>B. distachyon</i>, rice, or <i>A. thaliana.</i> A remarkable example of the abundance of dispersed duplicated genes in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome is provided by the <i>GLI1–GLU3</i> gene region on 1D. The region comprises 13 genes in the <i>B. distachyon</i>, rice, and sorghum genomes but it contains over 80 additional genes in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome, most of them dispersed duplicated genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Dong, L. et al. Rapid evolutionary dynamics in a 2.8-Mb chromosomal region containing multiple prolamin and resistance gene families in Aegilops tauschii. Plant J. 87, 495–506 (2016)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1742">22</a></sup>.</p><p>The density of HCC genes was highest (about 10–16 genes per Mb) in the distal chromosome regions, declining to about 2 genes per Mb in the proximal regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>), and was correlated with recombination rates (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig10">Extended Data Fig. 7a</a>). We provide here empirical evidence for the hypothesis that recombination rate is the causal variable in this relationship<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Dvorak, J. in Genetics and Genomics of the Triticeae Vol. 7 (eds Muehlbauer, G. &amp; Feuillet, C. ) 685–711 (Springer 2009)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1755">24</a></sup>. First, NCIs and end-to-end chromosome fusions reposition distal and proximal chromosome regions into new sites. This should perturb gene distribution around the NCI site<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="The International Brachypodium Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763–768 (2010)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1759">25</a></sup> but no such perturbations are apparent in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> pseudomolecules (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>). We propose that after an NCI or end-to-end fusion the repositioned region acquires a new recombination rate conforming to its new position relative to the telomere<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Devos, K. M., Dubcovsky, J., Dvořák, J., Chinoy, C. N. &amp; Gale, M. D. Structural evolution of wheat chromosomes 4A, 5A, and 7B and its impact on recombination. Theor. Appl. Genet. 91, 282–288 (1995)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1770">26</a></sup>. The new recombination rate alters the rate with which dispensable DNA is deleted from the region<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Dvorak, J., Yang, Z.-L., You, F. M. &amp; Luo, M. C. Deletion polymorphism in wheat chromosome regions with contrasting recombination rates. Genetics 168, 1665–1675 (2004)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1774">17</a></sup>, thereby altering gene density. Second, chromosome 6D is one of two <i>Ae. tauschii</i> chromosomes that have not been involved in an NCI or end-to-end chromosome fusion (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig5">Extended Data Fig. 2b</a>). The gene density and recombination rate profiles show collocated major and minor peaks (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>). Our proposal of recombination rate as the causal variable readily accounts for the peaks in both profiles. The major peaks in the recombination rate profile are caused by the preference of first crossovers for distal chromosome regions and the minor peaks are caused by the displacement of the rare second crossovers by crossover interference into the proximal regions. The peaks in gene density will evolve by the first process described above. However, if we assume that gene density is the causal variable, we can find no explanation for the evolution of the peaks.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Recombination rates, gene density, and gene colinearity."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Recombination rates, gene density, and gene colinearity.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature24486/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature24486/MediaObjects/41586_2017_BFnature24486_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature24486/MediaObjects/41586_2017_BFnature24486_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="417" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Relationship between gene density (red) and meiotic recombination rate (blue) for pseudomolecule 6D. The black triangle depicts the centromere. HCC gene set v1.0 was used to generate this figure. A similar pattern is observed in figures generated with HCC gene set v2.0 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig10">Extended Data Fig. 7a</a>). <b>b</b>, Synteny between the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> pseudomolecules and those of <i>B. distachyon</i> (Bd), rice (Os), sorghum (Sb), and self-syntenic blocks within the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> (Aet) genome. In the three interspecific comparisons, the absence of synteny in the centromere-adjacent regions of the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> pseudomolecules is reflected by empty space in those regions. Within the Aet genome, the largest syntenic regions are 1D with 3D and 6D with 7D. <b>c</b>, The percentage of genes in 50-gene intervals along the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> pseudomolecule 6D that are colinear with genes along homeologous pseudomolecules of <i>B. distachyon</i>, rice, and sorghum. The great similarity among the profiles indicates that they all reflect decay of colinearity along chromosome 6D. Note that the colinearity profiles are broadly an inverse of the recombination rate profile.</p><p>
<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#MOESM8">PowerPoint slide</a>
</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature24486/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The analysis of the distribution of HCC genes along the pseudomolecules allows us to extend our hypothesis that <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genes occur in small clusters (insulae)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Gottlieb, A. et al. Insular organization of gene space in grass genomes. PLoS One 8, e54101 (2013)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1850">27</a></sup>, which was previously based on the sequencing of several regions of the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome, to encompass the entire genome. We rejected the null hypothesis that genes are uniformly distributed along the chromosomes with <i>P</i> &lt; 0.001 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig10">Extended Data Fig. 7c</a>), and confirmed that the increase in gene density in distal regions is primarily caused by shortening of the inter-insular distances<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Gottlieb, A. et al. Insular organization of gene space in grass genomes. PLoS One 8, e54101 (2013)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1864">27</a></sup>.</p><p>A large number of simple sequence repeats (SSRs) were discovered in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig11">Extended Data Fig. 8a, c, d</a>), and represent a resource for the development of SSR markers for wheat genetics and breeding. We designed a portal (<a href="http://aegilops.wheat.ucdavis.edu/ATGSP/data.php">http://aegilops.wheat.ucdavis.edu/ATGSP/data.php</a>, at the ‘SSR search database’ link) to allow users to search for SSRs in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome sequence, facilitating the development of SSR markers. In the unmasked genome sequence, SSRs were more abundant in the distal, high-recombination regions than in the proximal low-recombination regions and their density correlated with recombination rates (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig11">Extended Data Fig. 8b</a>).</p><p>About 0.06% and 0.19% of the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome sequence consisted of chloroplast DNA and mitochondrial DNA insertions, respectively. These insertions correlated with recombination rates (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig12">Extended Data Fig. 9</a>).</p><p>The <i>Ae. tauschii</i> pseudomolecules showed synteny with those of <i>B. distachyon</i>, rice, and sorghum, except for the pericentromeric regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>), possibly reflecting rapid rearrangements of heterochromatic centromeric DNA in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome, as previously reported for the sorghum genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Bowers, J. E. et al. Comparative physical mapping links conservation of microsynteny to chromosome structure and recombination in grasses. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 13206–13211 (2005)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1915">28</a></sup>. Short self-syntenic blocks were detected within the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome, undoubtedly reflecting the pan-grass whole-genome duplication (WGD)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Paterson, A. H., Bowers, J. E. &amp; Chapman, B. A. Ancient polyploidization predating divergence of the cereals, and its consequences for comparative genomics. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 101, 9903–9908 (2004)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1923">29</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>) and comprised 2,839 (7.3%) HCC genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 5d</a>).</p><p>Colinearity of the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> HCC genes with genes along the <i>B. distachyon</i>, rice, and sorghum pseudomolecules ranged from 37.8% with <i>B. distachyon</i> to 33.7% with sorghum (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig13">Extended Data Fig. 10a</a>). Colinearity was the highest (about 60–70% of the HCC genes) in proximal, low-recombination regions of the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> pseudomolecules, and the lowest (about 10–20% of the HCC genes) in distal, high-recombination regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig10">Extended Data Fig. 7b</a>); colinearity and recombination rate were negatively correlated in all chromosomes except for 4D (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig10">Extended Data Figs 7a, b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig13">10b</a>).</p><p>To study further the influence of recombination on gene colinearity, colinearity profiles of rice pseudomolecules with those of <i>Ae. tauschii</i>, <i>B. distachyon</i>, and sorghum were developed. We predicted that the profiles would differ from the profiles obtained for <i>Ae. tauschii</i> because recombination rates along the rice chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Wu, J. et al. Physical maps and recombination frequency of six rice chromosomes. Plant J. 36, 720–730 (2003)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1976">30</a></sup> do not show the U-shape distribution characteristic of the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig10">Extended Data Fig. 7a</a>). The rice colinearity profiles indeed differed. They showed minimal colinearity in proximal regions and colinearity either increased or remained constant towards chromosome termini (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig13">Extended Data Fig. 10c</a>).</p><p>Since the divergence of Triticeae and Brachypodieae about 35 Ma<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="The International Brachypodium Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763–768 (2010)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d49203e1993">25</a></sup>, the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome acquired 350 chromosome rearrangements, of which 178 were large (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig13">Extended Data Fig. 10e</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#MOESM1">Supplementary Data 1</a>). Compared to the rice and sorghum genomes, the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome has been structurally evolving many fold faster (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig13">Extended Data Fig. 10e</a>). The differences in the accumulation of structural changes among the genomes are also apparent in dot plots (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig13">Extended Data Fig. 10d</a>). The <i>B. distachyon</i> lineage and the Pooideae branch preceding the Triticeae–Brachypodieae split were also relatively fast evolving, but the rate has further accelerated in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> lineage after the Triticeae–Brachypodieae split (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig13">Extended Data Fig. 10e</a>).</p><p>Fast structural evolution is accompanied by exceptional amounts of dispersed duplicated genes in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome. It is tempting to attribute these characteristics to the large size of the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome and the abundance of repeated sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Luo, M. C. et al. Genome comparisons reveal a dominant mechanism of chromosome number reduction in grasses and accelerated genome evolution in Triticeae. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 15780–15785 (2009)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2034">11</a></sup>. In that case, conifer genomes, which are much larger than the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome and contain large amounts of TEs, should be even more dynamic than the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome. Yet, conifer genomes are known for their stability<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Nystedt, B. et al. The Norway spruce genome sequence and conifer genome evolution. Nature 497, 579–584 (2013)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2045">31</a></sup>. We propose that it is not the size and TE content that sets the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome apart from other genomes, including the large pine genome, but the exceptionally high amount of very similar TEs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>), which lead to frequent recombination errors and cause gene duplications and other structural chromosome changes. A result of this is fast genome evolution, particularly at the ends of the chromosomes, with an abundance of duplicated genes and fast decay of synteny.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Plants</h3><p>Detailed information about <i>Ae. tauschii</i> accessions used in this project, their photos, taxonomy of <i>Ae. tauschii</i>, and its relationship to wheat can be found on our website (<a href="http://aegilops.wheat.ucdavis.edu/ATGSP/">http://aegilops.wheat.ucdavis.edu/ATGSP/</a>). In brief, <i>Ae. tauschii</i> accession AL8/78 was provided by V. Jaaska, who collected it near the Hrazdan River, Jerevan, Armenia. The accession is classified as <i>Ae. tauschii</i> ssp. <i>strangulata.</i> We selected this accession for the construction of BAC libraries and the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> physical map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Luo, M. C. et al. A 4-gigabase physical map unlocks the structure and evolution of the complex genome of Aegilops tauschii, the wheat D-genome progenitor. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 110, 7940–7945 (2013)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2093">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Luo, M. C. et al. in Proc. 10th Internatl Wheat Genet. Symp. (Eds Pogna, N. E., Romano, M., Pogna, E. A. &amp; Galterio, G. ) 293–296 (S.IM.I., 2003)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2096">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Luo, M. C. et al. High-throughput fingerprinting of bacterial artificial chromosomes using the snapshot labeling kit and sizing of restriction fragments by capillary electrophoresis. Genomics 82, 378–389 (2003)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2099">33</a></sup> because of its genetic proximity to the wheat D genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Dvorak, J., Luo, M. C., Yang, Z. L. &amp; Zhang, H. -B. The structure of the Aegilops tauschii genepool and the evolution of hexaploid wheat. Theor. Appl. Genet. 97, 657–670 (1998)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2103">34</a></sup>. The accession was also used for the construction of a genome-wide optical BioNano genome (BNG) map. We maintain this accession, which can be requested from the corresponding author (J.D.).</p><p>A more recent study of genetic relationships between <i>Ae. tauschii</i> and wheat uncovered a group of accessions in Caspian Iran that appeared even more closely related to the wheat D genome than AL8/78<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Wang, J. et al. Aegilops tauschii single nucleotide polymorphisms shed light on the origins of wheat D-genome genetic diversity and pinpoint the geographic origin of hexaploid wheat. New Phytol. 198, 925–937 (2013)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2113">35</a></sup>. They belong to <i>Ae. tauschii</i> ssp. <i>tauschii</i> var. <i>meyeri.</i> We selected from this population accession CIae23<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Wang, J. et al. Aegilops tauschii single nucleotide polymorphisms shed light on the origins of wheat D-genome genetic diversity and pinpoint the geographic origin of hexaploid wheat. New Phytol. 198, 925–937 (2013)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2127">35</a></sup> for the construction of the second <i>Ae. tauschii</i> BNG map. CIae23 was made available by the US National Plant Germplasm System.</p><p>The third accession relevant to this project is wheat (<i>T. aestivum</i>) cv. Chinese Spring. We used accession DV418, which is derived from a colchicine-doubled haploid maintained by J.D. at UC Davis, and used it for the construction of a genome-wide BNG map of wheat.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Illumina MiSeq BAC sequencing</h3><p>The minimal tiling path across 3,578 BAC contigs consisted of 42,822 <i>Ae. tauschii</i> AL8/78 BAC clones<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Luo, M. C. et al. A 4-gigabase physical map unlocks the structure and evolution of the complex genome of Aegilops tauschii, the wheat D-genome progenitor. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 110, 7940–7945 (2013)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2151">7</a></sup>. The minimal tiling path clones were re-arrayed according to the chromosome and BAC contig to which they belonged. Both ends of each BAC clone were sequenced with the ABI 3730XL platform<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Kelley, J. M. et al. High throughput direct end sequencing of BAC clones. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 1539–1546 (1999)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2155">36</a></sup>. Minimal tiling path clones and 2,000 singleton BAC clones were allocated into 5,646 pools averaging eight, usually overlapping, BAC clones.</p><p>To isolate BAC DNA for sequencing, 1 ml of LB liquid medium containing chloramphenicol (12.5 μg ml<sup>−1</sup>) was inoculated with 10 μl of culture from a single well of the re-arrayed BAC library, and cells were grown at 37 °C for approximately 6 h while shaking. The culture was visually checked for sufficient growth and then combined with an additional 9 ml of LB liquid medium containing chloramphenicol (12.5 μg ml<sup>−1</sup>). The 10-ml culture was grown for another 16 h at 37 °C while shaking. The 10-ml cultures were visually checked for uniform growth by comparing them with each other.</p><p>A BAC pool was created, using cultures with sufficient turbidity, by combining six to ten, but typically eight, 10-ml cultures of overlapping clones from a single contig. The cultures that did not have sufficient turbidity were regrown. Those that repeatedly failed were replaced by alternative BAC clones while maintaining a minimal tiling path across the BAC contig. The pooled cultures were centrifuged for 15 min at 2,000<i>g</i> in a Sorvall centrifuge with a SA600 rotor and pellets were stored at −20 °C until a sufficient quantity of pools was available for DNA isolation. DNA was isolated from the BAC pools with the Qiagen midiprep kit protocol (Qiagen 12145), modified by including an ATP-dependent exonuclease (Qiagen) in the DNA digestion step before final column purification and by not using the pre-column provided in the kit. BAC pool DNA was sheared using the Covaris system (duty, 5%; intensity, 3; cycles per burst, 200; 20 s) and purified with the Qiagen MinElute PCR purification kit (Qiagen 28006).</p><p>Illumina libraries were constructed from 1 μg of sheared fragments of BAC-pool DNA with the KAPA library preparation kit (Kapa Biosystems) following the manufacturer’s protocol. The libraries were normalized and 48 BAC pools were combined for one 600-cycle sequencing run with MiSeq reagent kit v3. Each read was allocated to a pool on the basis of its index.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Scaffold assembly</h3><p>A total of 90,050 BAC-end sequences were generated, of which 80,906 contained both forward and reverse reads with lengths of at least 63 bp. These pairs were aligned to the scaffolds and used as an assembly validation step.</p><p>Each BAC pool contained Illumina MiSeq data from approximately eight BAC clones (ranging from a minimum of six to a maximum of ten). Most BAC clones overlapped (see above). The average overlap between adjacent BAC clones was ~25 kb, and the overall genomic span for each pool averaged ~1 Mb.</p><p>Prior to assembly, all reads were trimmed to remove any known Illumina vector and adaptor sequences. Bacterial plasmids, <i>Escherichia coli</i> sequences, and phage ΦX174 sequences were also removed. The pool reads were then assembled using SOAPdenovo2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Luo, R. B. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 1–6 (2012)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2189">37</a></sup> generating an initial set of contigs and scaffolds.</p><p>From the initial assemblies, any scaffolds with unusually low coverage were identified and removed as likely artefacts. The remaining scaffolds were aligned to each other, and those that were completely contained within other scaffolds in the same pool were removed.</p><p>To improve the pool assemblies further, several paired-end libraries from a previously published WGS dataset<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Jia, J. et al. Aegilops tauschii draft genome sequence reveals a gene repertoire for wheat adaptation. Nature 496, 91–95 (2013)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2199">5</a></sup> were used. This dataset included 84 libraries, from which we used six libraries of mate-paired reads from fragments ranging in length from 1.6 to 8.6 kb, with an average read length of 89 bp. These WGS libraries provided long-range linking information for the construction of scaffolds. The WGS reads were mapped to the initial assemblies using nucmer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2203">38</a></sup>. Mate-paired reads that mapped to a pool were added to the dataset of the pool. These augmented data were then re-scaffolded.</p><p>The total length of the scaffolds in this assembly (Aet v1.0, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 1b</a>) was 5.79 Gb, exceeding the estimated genome size of 4.02 Gb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Arumuganathan, K. &amp; Earle, E. D. Nuclear DNA content of some important plant species. Plant Mol. Biol. Report. 9, 208–218 (1991)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2213">39</a></sup> by 44%. Assembly Aet v1.0 contained 250,177 scaffolds with an N50 scaffold size of 207,812 bp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 1b</a>). This included 96,546 scaffolds with a size of 2,000 bp or longer for a total length of 5.71 Gb. Pools that overlapped unambiguously with one another were merged. To merge the pools, all scaffolds of pools within a chromosome were aligned to one another using nucmer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2220">38</a></sup> and then the minimus2 assembler, a modified version of minimus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Sommer, D. D., Delcher, A. L., Salzberg, S. L. &amp; Pop, M. Minimus: a fast, lightweight genome assembler. BMC Bioinformatics 8, 64 (2007)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2224">40</a></sup> designed for merging assemblies. If BAC-end sequences (which were also mapped to chromosomes) indicated that two scaffolds should overlap, they were merged regardless of their chromosome origin. Scaffolds were considered for merging only if they overlapped by at least 2,000 bp with at least 99% identity. We chose these relatively permissive conditions for merging because we had the ability to detect misassembled scaffolds downstream by scaffold alignment on the BNG map contigs. Scaffold merging increased the N50 to 410,889 bp and decreased the total length of the assembly to 4.46 Gb. This assembly was named Aet v1.1 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 1b</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">WGS reads and scaffolds</h3><p>An independent WGS assembly was executed to increase the lengths of scaffolds and close gaps in the Aet v1.1 assembly. Five WGS genomic libraries were constructed (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 1c</a>) and sequenced with an Illumina HiSeq 2500 sequencer at the Roy J. Carver Biotechnology Center, Urbana, Illinois, which produced 1.05 Tb of sequence. A total of 191× coverage in reads was employed in an assembly with the software package DenovoMAGIC2; no other sequence data were used in this process. The main steps of DenovoMAGIC2 (NRGene) were as follows.</p><p>(1) Read pre-processing and error correction. PCR duplicates, Illumina adaptors, and linkers were removed. Also removed were paired-end reads (PERs) that likely contained sequencing errors. These included all reads with sub-sequences of ≥23 bp not found in at least one other independent read. PERs with ≥10 bp sequence overlap were merged using FLASH41 to create ‘stitched reads’.</p><p>(2) Contig assembly. The stitched reads were used to build a de Bruijn graph<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Pevzner, P. A., Tang, H. &amp; Waterman, M. S. An Eulerian path approach to DNA fragment assembly. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 98, 9748–9753 (2001)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2248">42</a></sup>, from which contigs were constructed using a <i>k</i>-mer of 191 bp. By walking through the graph, the software identified non-repetitive contigs and used stitched reads to resolve repeats and extend non-repetitive sequences of contigs where possible.</p><p>These steps were similar to those in DenovoMAGIC1, which was used to assemble the PH207 <i>Zea mays</i> inbred line<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Hirsch, C. N. et al. Draft assembly of elite inbred line PH207 provides insights into genomic and transcriptome diversity in maize. Plant Cell 28, 2700–2714 (2016)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2261">43</a></sup>, but with the following key differences. DenovoMAGIC2 takes advantage of improvements in Illumina sequencing technology and requires more sequencing; notably a minimum of 60× coverage in 2 × 250 bp reads versus ~20× in DenovoMAGIC1, and ~30× coverage in the longer PERs (compared to their absence in DenovoMAGIC1). Another change was the elimination of the requirement for TruSeq (or ‘Moleculo’) libraries from the data input. Importantly, the effective read length was increased by merging (when possible, based on ≥10 bp overlap) the 2 × 250bp reads from the short insert size PERs library. This merge generated relatively long, high quality reads (over 400 bp) with a coverage of about 50× genome equivalents. Another key change in the algorithm was the capability to support large <i>k</i>-mers (up to 191 bp, as used here) for the DeBrujn graph. The large <i>k</i>-mer size substantially reduced the complexity of the DeBruijn graph.</p><p>(3) Scaffold assembly. Scaffolding by DenovoMAGIC2 used the same algorithm as was used in the maize assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Hirsch, C. N. et al. Draft assembly of elite inbred line PH207 provides insights into genomic and transcriptome diversity in maize. Plant Cell 28, 2700–2714 (2016)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2275">43</a></sup>.</p><p>(4) Gap filling. A final step filled gaps using PER and mate-paired links, along with de Bruijn graph analysis to detect instances where a unique path of reads spanned a gap.</p><p>This WGS assembly, designated Aet WGS v1.0, contained scaffolds with an N50 of 1,098,654 bp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 1b</a>). NRGene performed another assembly (Aet WGS v1.1) later using another 8–10 kb mated-paired Illumina library. This assembly generated longer scaffolds (N50 = 11,362,824 bp) and these were used in super-scaffolding (see section Super-scaffolding and pseudomolecule construction).</p><p>We also used 35× <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome coverage of long Pacific Biosciences (PacBio) WGS reads produced with the PacBio RSII platform at John Hopkins Genome Center. PacBio reads have a high error rate. Errors in the reads were corrected with 32.4× Illumina WGS reads (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 1c</a>). These WGS hybrid mega-reads<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Zimin, A. V. et al. Hybrid assembly of the large and highly repetitive genome of Aegilops tauschii, a progenitor of bread wheat, with the MaSuRCA mega-reads algorithm. Genome Res. 27, 787–792 (2017)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2296">6</a></sup> were used in scaffold merging (see below).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Scaffold merging</h3><p>The WGS assembly Aet WGS v1.0 had longer scaffolds but much shorter contigs than the BAC pool assembly Aet v1.1. We merged the Aet v1.1 and Aet WGS v1.0 assemblies in several steps as follows.</p><p>(1) The MaSuRCA genome assembler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Zimin, A. V. et al. The MaSuRCA genome assembler. Bioinformatics 29, 2669–2677 (2013)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2311">44</a></sup> gap-closing module was used to fill intra-scaffold gaps in the Aet WGS v1.0 assembly. The gaps were filled using the same Illumina reads used to build the Aet WGS v1.0 assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 1b</a>). The MaSuRCA gap-closing module used original, untrimmed and uncorrected Illumina WGS reads to fill gaps in scaffolds. The gap-closing module<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Zimin, A. V. et al. The MaSuRCA genome assembler. Bioinformatics 29, 2669–2677 (2013)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2318">44</a></sup> maps the reads to the sequences flanking the gaps and attempts to build a unique path of <i>k</i>-mers closing each gap, varying the <i>k</i>-mer size from 21 to 127. This closed more than 300,000 gaps and reduced the number of contigs to 525,538, increasing the contig N50 size from 16.4 to 29.2 kb.</p><p>(2) Four ‘artificial’ mate-paired libraries of lengths 5, 12, 25, and 50 Kb were generated from the BAC pool assembly, Aet v1.1. Paired-end reads were produced every 500 bp across the assembly, producing 7–10 million pairs per library. These pairs were then used along with the SOAPdenovo2 scaffolder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Luo, R. B. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler.
