Gigascience 1, 1–6 (2012)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2331">37</a></sup> to re-scaffold the assembly from the previous step.</p><p>(3) These improved scaffolds were aligned to all scaffolds in the BAC-based assembly Aet v1.1, which had larger contigs and hence much better contiguity. These alignments identified many contigs in Aet v1.1 that spanned gaps in the WGS assembly, and we used the Aet v1.1 sequence to fill these gaps.</p><p>Steps 1–3 closed 560,000 gaps (83% of the total), reducing their number from 671,689 to 111,690 and estimated length from 178 to 49 Mb. These gap-closing steps increased the contig N50 size, from 16.4 to 88.3 kb.</p><p>(4) The dataset of hybrid PacBio/Illumina WGS mega-reads (previous section) was used in gap closing with the MaSuRCA gap-closing module. This step closed an additional 6,761 gaps, which increased the N50 contig size from 88.3 kb to 92.5 kb.</p><p>The assembly at this point contained a substantial number of small (&lt;10 kb) scaffolds that were completely contained in other contigs or scaffolds. These were identified by aligning all scaffolds back to the assembly using bwa-mem<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate long-read alignment with Burrows–Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 26, 589–595 (2010)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2345">45</a></sup>. A total of 51,520 scaffolds that were at least 99.5% identical to and contained within other scaffolds were removed. The result of this step was assembly Aet v2.0, with a scaffold N50 of 2,884,388 bp and contig N50 of 93,210 bp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 1b</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Optical BNG map construction</h3><p>Etiolated leaves were collected from greenhouse-grown young plants of <i>Ae. tauschii</i> accessions AL8/78 and CIae23, and hexaploid wheat Chinese Spring, accession DV418, and mailed to Amplicon Express (Pullman) for isolation of high-molecular-mass DNA. The size of fragments produced by Amplicon Express ranged from 0.7 to 1 Mb, which greatly exceeded the sizes of fragments we were able to produce (0.3–0.4 Mb). These large fragments were central for the construction of BNG maps with large contig sizes and genome coverage. The nicking site frequencies for Nt.BspQI, Nb.BsmI, Nb.BbvCI, and Nb.BsrDI in the genome of <i>Ae. tauschii</i> acc. AL8/78 were estimated with the software Knickers (<a href="http://www.bnxinstall.com/knickers/Knickers.htm">http://www.bnxinstall.com/knickers/Knickers.htm</a>). Nt.BspQI (New England BioLabs) generated approximately 15 nicks per 100 kb, which was close to the optimum frequency, and was selected for further work. The DNA molecules were nicked with Nt.BspQI and the nicks were labelled according to the instructions provided with the IrysPrep Reagent Kit (BioNano Genomics), and as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Lam, E. T. et al. Genome mapping on nanochannel arrays for structural variation analysis and sequence assembly. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 771–776 (2012)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2373">46</a></sup>. The labelled DNA sample was loaded onto the IrysChip nanochannel array (BioNano Genomics). The stretched DNA molecules were imaged with the Irys imaging system (BioNano Genomics). Raw image data were converted into bnx files and from these, the AutoDetect software (BioNano Genomics) generated basic labelling and DNA length information. The DNA molecules in bnx format were then aligned against each other. Clusters were formed and assembled into contigs with a BioNano Genomics assembly pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Lam, E. T. et al. Genome mapping on nanochannel arrays for structural variation analysis and sequence assembly. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 771–776 (2012)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2377">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Cao, H. Z. et al. Rapid detection of structural variation in a human genome using nanochannel-based genome mapping technology. Gigascience 3, 34 (2014)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2380">47</a></sup>. The <i>P</i> value thresholds were optimized for pairwise assembly, extension/refinement, and final refinement for each genome. For each assembly, an initial assembly was produced and checked for chimaeric contigs. Assembly parameters were adjusted if necessary and assembly was repeated. Ultimately, BNG maps for <i>Ae. tauschii</i> accessions AL8/78 and CIae23, and for Chinese Spring wheat were generated.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Validation of scaffolds with the AL8/78 BNG map and resolution of chimaeras</h3><p>To compare sequence assemblies with the AL8/78 BNG map, the Aet v2.0 scaffolds were digested <i>in silico</i> with Nt.BspQI nickase using the program Knickers and aligned on the BNG map with RefAligner. The alignments were visualized in IrysView. Knickers, RefAligner, and IrysView were obtained from BioNano Genomics (<a href="https://bionanogenomics.com/support/software-downloads/">https://bionanogenomics.com/support/software-downloads/</a>). Of 111,834 scaffolds in the Aet v2.0 assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 1b</a>), <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig5">2</a>,295 were of sufficient length to be validated by the BNG map. Of these, 120 were chimaeric and were disjoined, which increased the total number of scaffolds to 111,973 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 1e</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Super-scaffolding</h3><p>Super-scaffolds were generated with the Stitch algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Shelton, J. M. et al. Tools and pipelines for BioNano data: molecule assembly pipeline and FASTA super scaffolding tool. BMC Genomics 16, 734 (2015)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2426">48</a></sup> using the AL8/78 BNG map contigs as guides. The filtering parameters of Stitch were trained to be suitable for the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> AL8/78 genome, and Stitch was performed in iterations until no additional super-scaffolds could be produced. After each round of Stitch, the ‘potentially chimaeric scaffolds’ flagged by the program were manually checked and resolved if necessary. Stitching reduced the 111,973 scaffolds to 110,527 super-scaffolds. In the next super-scaffolding step, we used 305 scaffolds of NRGene assembly WGS v1.1, which reduced the number of super-scaffolds to 109,861 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 1e</a>). The total length of these super-scaffolds was 4,224,918,192 bp. This was the Aet v3.0 assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 1b</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Assessment of the accuracy and completeness of assembly Aet v3.0</h3><p>Sequences of 195 BAC clones were downloaded from NCBI, where they were deposited as separate accessions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Massa, A. N. et al. Gene space dynamics during the evolution of Aegilops tauschii, Brachypodium distachyon, Oryza sativa, and Sorghum bicolor genomes. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 2537–2547 (2011)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2447">8</a></sup>. The clones were originally sequenced using Sanger sequencing technology and assembled with the Celera Assembler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Myers, E. W. et al. A whole-genome assembly of Drosophila. Science 287, 2196–2204 (2000)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2451">49</a></sup>. These BAC sequences contained 2,405 contigs of a total length of 25,540,177 bp. The contigs were aligned to Aet v3.0 as follows. The contigs in the Aet v3.0 were indexed and the 2,405 contigs were aligned to the Aet v3.0 assembly using bwa in its long-read mode<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate long-read alignment with Burrows–Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 26, 589–595 (2010)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2455">45</a></sup>. This procedure consistently aligned 2,180 contigs end-to-end to the contigs of Aet v3.0. The contigs were then re-aligned using nucmer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2459">38</a></sup> with more sensitive settings, and additional end-to-end and partial alignments were identified. All alignments not matching end to end were inspected to determine whether they contained misassemblies.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Pseudomolecule assembly</h3><p>Of the 109,861 super-scaffolds in Aet v3.0 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 1b</a>), 283 (accounting for 95.2% of the total sequence) were anchored on the SNP-based genetic map comprising 7,185 SNP markers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Luo, M. C. et al. A 4-gigabase physical map unlocks the structure and evolution of the complex genome of Aegilops tauschii, the wheat D-genome progenitor. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 110, 7940–7945 (2013)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2474">7</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 1f</a>). The remaining 107,888 super-scaffolds were short and totaled only 199,614,049 bp, accounting for 4.8% of the total 4,224,918,192 bp sequence. Of the 283 large super-scaffolds, the order or orientation of 81 (28.6%) was uncertain (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 1f</a>, step 1).</p><p>The use of a BNG map for super-scaffolding and pseudomolecule construction is limited by gaps in the map, which are caused by chance clustering of Nt.BspQI nickase restriction sites, making such regions prone to breakage during DNA labelling and electrophoresis. Polymorphism for Nt.BspQI sites can alter the distribution of these fragile regions, and contigs of different BNG maps may therefore overlap. These overlaps could be used to bridge some of the gaps on individual BNG maps, provided that such maps can be aligned. On the basis of this hypothesis, BNG maps were constructed for <i>Ae. tauschii</i> acc. CIae23 and <i>T. aestivum</i> cv. Chinese Spring (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 1d</a>). Contigs of these BNG maps were aligned with the 283 super-scaffolds and AL8/78 BNG contigs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 1g</a>). Polymorphism among the three genotypes did not prevent contig alignments but was sufficient to alter the locations of some of the fragile sites. The use of these two additional BNG maps improved the ordering and orienting of super-scaffolds on the pseudomolecules, leaving only 16 (5.6%) of the 283 super-scaffolds unordered (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 1f</a>, step 2). These remaining 16 super-scaffolds were known to be located in the centromeric region on the basis of SNP marker anchoring. However, because the anchoring markers co-segregated, the order and orientation of those super-scaffolds remained unresolved.</p><p>To construct pseudomolecules, the gaps between the neighbouring super-scaffolds were filled with 1,000 Ns. Each pseudomolecule started at the tip of the short chromosome arm. The pseudomolecules contained 95.2% of assembled sequences with a total length of 4,025,304,143 bp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig5">Extended Data Fig. 2a</a>). The unanchored super-scaffolds were short and accounted for about 200 Mb. A total of 76 Mb of BNG contigs were devoid of aligned scaffolds. The pseudomolecules and unanchored super-scaffolds comprise assembly Aet v4.0.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">TE annotation</h3><p>TEs were identified by discovering full-length TEs using structure-based analyses and by scanning the Aet v4.0 assembly with newly identified and previously known TEs to find all (full-length and truncated) elements by RepeatMasker (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>). A full-length TE is defined as an element with a complete 5′–3′ sequence that includes terminals flanked by target site duplications. For <i>helitrons</i>, which have no target site duplications, a 5′-A and 3′-T were required to flank the termini of an element. The structure-based bioinformatic tools used were LTR_FINDER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265–268 (2007)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2527">50</a></sup> and LTRharvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinformatics 9, 18 (2008)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2531">51</a></sup> to find LTR-RTs, SINE-Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Wenke, T. et al. Targeted identification of short interspersed nuclear element families shows their widespread existence and extreme heterogeneity in plant genomes. Plant Cell 23, 3117–3128 (2011)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2535">52</a></sup> to find SINEs, MITE Hunter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Han, Y. J. &amp; Wessler, S. R. MITE-Hunter: a program for discovering miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements from genomic sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, e199 (2010)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2540">53</a></sup> to find miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements (MITEs), and Helitron Scanner<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Xiong, W., He, L., Lai, J., Dooner, H. K. &amp; Du, C. HelitronScanner uncovers a large overlooked cache of Helitron transposons in many plant genomes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, 10263–10268 (2014)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2544">54</a></sup> to find <i>Helitrons</i>. DNA elements with terminal inverted repeats (TIRs) were identified by integrating a homology search for DDE domains and identification of TIR insertion junctions. The genome was first scanned by BLASTP (<i>E</i>-value =10<sup>−10</sup>) using known DDE domains as a query; then, matched regions as well as their flanking sequences were grouped according to the domains they matched and the matched domains were extracted. For each group, closely related subgroups based on the similarity of DDE domains were subsequently identified. For each subgroup, corresponding DNA sequences were aligned and the alignments were inspected for insertion junctions and target site duplications, thus identifying full-length elements. All program outputs were manually inspected to eliminate artefacts. The verified, full-length TEs were then classified into families using previously described criteria<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Wicker, T. et al. A unified classification system for eukaryotic transposable elements. Nat. Rev. Genet. 8, 973–982 (2007)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2556">55</a></sup>. Elements within a family had &gt;80% sequence identity at the DNA level. We constructed an <i>Ae. tauschii</i> TE database combining all TE families and used that information to mask the pseudomolecules with RepeatMasker.</p><p>To identify new families, representative sequences were compared to known plant TEs in TREP (<a href="https://wheat.pw.usda.gov/">https://wheat.pw.usda.gov/</a>), PGSB Repeat Element Database (<a href="http://pgsb.helmholtz-muenchen.de/plant/recat/">http://pgsb.helmholtz-muenchen.de/plant/recat/</a>), and Repbase (<a href="http://www.girinst.org/repbase/">www.girinst.org/repbase/</a>), and families were classified based on their LTRs.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Age distribution and insertion rate of TE families</h3><p>Because mutations in LTRs occur at random, two identical LTR-RTs inserted at the same time could have different numbers of mutations. The numbers of mutations on a single LTR with length <i>l</i> and inserted <i>Y</i> years ago was assumed to follow a Poisson distribution with the rate <i>rlY</i>, in which <i>r</i> = 1.3 × 10<sup>−8</sup> mutations per year·per site<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Ma, J. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Rapid recent growth and divergence of rice nuclear genomes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 101, 12404–12410 (2004)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2611">56</a></sup>. The number of mismatches for a given fixed age (time since insertion) on a pair of LTRs is <i>N</i>|<i>Y</i>, which follows a Poisson distribution with rate 2<i>rlY.</i> Negative binomial distributions provided a reasonable approximation of the distributions of mismatches <i>N</i> in most of the TE families. By a known probabilistic relation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Leemis, L. M. &amp; McQueston, J. T. Univariate distribution relationships. Am. Stat. 62, 45–53 (2008)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2627">57</a></sup>, the age distribution of <i>Y</i> follows a gamma distribution. We then used the method of maximum likelihood to estimate the age distributions. The insertion rate <i>t</i> years ago was estimated by <img src="//media.springernature.com/lw139/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature24486/MediaObjects/41586_2017_BFnature24486_IEq1_HTML.gif" style="width:139px;max-width:none;" alt="" /> where <i>g</i>(<i>t</i>) was the density of TEs with age <i>t</i>, and <img src="//media.springernature.com/lw34/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature24486/MediaObjects/41586_2017_BFnature24486_IEq2_HTML.gif" style="width:34px;max-width:none;" alt="" /> was the fraction of surviving elements past age <i>t</i>, estimated by fitting an exponential curve to divergence.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Distribution of TEs along chromosome arms and their ages</h3><p>To determine whether the distribution of TE families along <i>Ae. tauschii</i> chromosomes was homogeneous, mean TE distances to the centromere for the 22 most abundant <i>Gypsy</i> and <i>Copia</i> families were computed. One-way ANOVA was performed to compare the mean distances of TEs to the centromeres. The global TE family effect was found to be highly significant (<i>P</i> &lt; 2 × 10<sup>−16</sup>). Tukey’s test was applied to determine which families were more proximal/distal (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig6">Extended Data Fig. 3c</a>).</p><p>The hypothesis that LTR-RT families along the centromere–telomere axes of chromosome arms have the same mean ages was tested by separating the 22 families on the basis of their median distance from the centromere into a proximal and distal group, each consisting of 11 families. The difference between the families was statistically tested as described in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig6">Extended Data Fig. 3c</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Gene annotation</h3><p>Genes were annotated by combining splice site-aware alignments of protein sequences from available public reference datasets with RNA sequencing (RNA-seq) assemblies and inferred putative open-reading frames (ORFs). The ORFs were classified as HCC or LCC on the basis of sequence homology and gene expression support.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Transcript prediction</h3><p>The gene annotation pipeline (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig7">Extended Data Fig. 4a</a>) combined information of splice site-aware alignments with reference proteins and RNA-seq-based gene structure predictions. Protein sequences from barley (<i>H. vulgare</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Mayer, K. F. et al. A physical, genetic and functional sequence assembly of the barley genome. Nature 491, 711–716 (2012)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2720">58</a></sup>, <i>B. distachyon</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="The International Brachypodium Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763–768 (2010)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2726">25</a></sup>, rice (<i>O. sativa</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="The Rice Chromosome 3 Sequencing Consortium. Sequence, annotation, and analysis of synteny between rice chromosome 3 and diverged grass species. Genome Res. 15, 1284–1291 (2005)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2734">59</a></sup>, and sorghum (<i>S. bicolor</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2741">18</a></sup> as well as predicted ORFs from full-length cDNA sequences from wheat (<i>T. aestivum</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Mochida, K., Yoshida, T., Sakurai, T., Ogihara, Y. &amp; Shinozaki, K. TriFLDB: a database of clustered full-length coding sequences from Triticeae with applications to comparative grass genomics. Plant Physiol. 150, 1135–1146 (2009)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2748">60</a></sup> were aligned against repeat-masked <i>Ae. tauschii</i> pseudomolecules using the splice-aware alignment software GenomeThreader (v.1.6.2; parameters used: -species rice -gcmincoverage 30 -prseedlength 7 -prhdist 4 -force). The resulting transcript structure predictions were then merged using Cuffcompare from the Cufflinks package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protocols 7, 562–578 (2014)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2756">61</a></sup>. The RNA-seq transcriptome data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig7">Extended Data Fig. 4b</a>) contained reads from 24 <i>Ae. tauschii</i> samples that were published previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Jia, J. et al. Aegilops tauschii draft genome sequence reveals a gene repertoire for wheat adaptation. Nature 496, 91–95 (2013)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2766">5</a></sup> (Sequence Read Archive, SRA062662, accessions SRR630112 through SRR630135). Additional RNA-seq data (paired end 150-bp reads, quality trimmed to paired end 100-bp reads) were generated by us from <i>Ae. tauschii</i> AL8/78 leaf and root tissues from 2-week-old, greenhouse-grown plants, 4-day seedling tissues, developing seeds (10 and 27 days after anthesis) and pooled RNA from developing grains at 10, 15, 20, 27, and 30 days after anthesis. Tri-reagent was used for RNA isolation from leaf and root tissues, and LiCl and acid phenol were used to extract RNA from developing grains<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Oñate-Sánchez, L. &amp; Vicente-Carbajosa, J. DNA-free RNA isolation protocols for Arabidopsis thaliana, including seeds and siliques. BMC Res. Notes 1, 93 (2008)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2773">62</a></sup>.</p><p>The RNA-seq reads were aligned against the repeat-masked pseudomolecules using TopHat2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protocols 7, 562–578 (2014)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2780">61</a></sup> with default parameters. The resulting alignment files were then assembled into transcript structures using the Cufflinks software package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protocols 7, 562–578 (2014)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2784">61</a></sup>. The RNA-seq-based transcript structures were clustered with the reference-based gene model predictions to generate a consensus transcript set using Cuffcompare from the Cufflinks package.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">ORF prediction and selection</h3><p>A custom script was used to extract transcript sequences on the basis of their coordinates. Transdecoder (version rel_16Jan; parameters: -m 30 –retain_long_orfs 90 –search_pfam pfam.AB.hmm.bin) was applied to determine putative open reading frames as well as the corresponding protein sequences including prediction of PFAM domains. For some transcripts, alternative protein predictions were obtained. All predicted proteins were therefore compared by BLASTP with a comprehensive protein database that contained high confidence protein sequences from <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2798">63</a></sup>, <i>Z. mays</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112–1115 (2009)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2804">64</a></sup>, <i>B. distachyon</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="The International Brachypodium Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763–768 (2010)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2810">25</a></sup>, rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2814">65</a></sup> and sorghum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2819">18</a></sup>. BLASTP hits with an <i>E</i>-value below 10<sup>−5</sup> were considered significant hits. Sequential filtering was then used to find a single best translation for each transcript. The filtering steps were: (1) with homology support, without homology support but with PFAM domains, and with neither homology support nor PFAM domains; (2) total length of translation; (3) coding sequence (CDS) with start and with stop codon, CDS with start and without stop codon, CDS without start and with stop codon, and CDS without start and without stop codon; (4) number of PFAM domains; and (5) number of significant BLAST hits.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Confidence assignment</h3><p>The predicted genes were subjected to stringent confidence classification to discriminate between loci representing HCC protein-coding genes and less reliable LCC genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 5a</a>), which included gene fragments, putative pseudogenes, and non(-protein)-coding transcripts. Confidence was assigned to a gene in two steps using the criteria and methods described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Mayer, K. F. X. et al. A chromosome-based draft sequence of the hexaploid bread wheat (Triticum aestivum) genome. Science 345, 1251788 (2014)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2840">66</a></sup>. First, genes with transcripts that showed significant homology (BLASTN with <i>E</i>-value &lt; 10<sup>−10</sup>) to a repeat element library were considered as low confidence. Second, the predicted peptide sequences were compared with the protein datasets of wheat, barley, <i>B. distachyon</i>, rice, sorghum, and <i>A. thaliana</i> using BLASTP and hits with homology below 10<sup>−10</sup> were considered as significant. For each gene, the best-matching reference protein was selected as a template and the transcript sequence with maximum coverage of the template was defined as a gene representative. Genes were defined as HCC if their representative protein had a similarity to the respective template above a threshold (&gt;60% for <i>A. thaliana</i>, sorghum, and rice, &gt;65% for <i>B. distachyon</i>, and &gt;87% for barley and wheat). This process generated annotation v1.0 and the HCC gene set v1.0 containing 28,847 genes.</p><p>To broaden the search for wheat-specific genes and genes with reduced sequence homology but high expression that may have been placed into the LCC class in annotation v1.0, all LCC gene models were re-classified. All predicted protein sequences, including all potential isoforms, were compared with a database containing Triticeae protein sequences from UniProt (downloaded 20 February 2017, filtered for complete sequences) using BLASTP (v.2.60). LCC genes with a significant alignment (<i>E</i>-value &lt; 10<sup>−10</sup>, overlap &gt; 95%) to an annotated Triticeae protein were transferred into the HCC class. To include also highly expressed <i>Ae. tauschii</i> specific genes, we used Hisat2 (v.2.06) and Stringtie (v.1.3.3) to quantify gene expression in single samples. LCC genes with low sequence homology to reference genomes but strong expression in at least one sample (FPKM &gt; 1) were also considered as additional HCC genes. Finally, 77 LCC genes that were manually identified as RGAs and manually curated gene structures from the prolamin gene family were transferred from LCC into the HCC class. This reclassification and adding additional, manually curated genes produced an annotation v2.0 with updated HCC gene set v2.0 containing 39,622 genes.</p><p>Most subsequent gene analyses were performed with both HCC gene sets. As the results were similar, only those generated with the more inclusive HCC gene set v2.0 will be described, unless it is specifically stated that HCC gene set v1.0 was used.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Validation of annotation</h3><p>The completeness of gene annotation was evaluated by searching the entire annotation v1.0 with a set of 956 BUSCO genes and BUSCO software (early release, database: plantdb, <a href="http://busco.ezlab.org/">http://busco.ezlab.org/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Simão, F. A. et al. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics (2015)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2892">19</a></sup>. Although 98.4% of the BUSCO genes were correctly annotated, only 90% were correctly assigned to the HCC class. The updated annotation v2.0 was validated with BUSCO v2 (database: embryophyta odb9 containing 1440 BUSCO genes) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 5b</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Duplicate gene prediction</h3><p>The program duplicate_gene_classifier from MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Wang, Y., Li, J. &amp; Paterson, A. H. MCScanX-transposed: detecting transposed gene duplications based on multiple colinearity scans. Bioinformatics 29, 1458–1460 (2013)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2907">67</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Wang, Y. P. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2910">68</a></sup> was used to detect duplicate genes and classify them as either tandem or dispersed duplicated genes. Tandem duplicated genes were defined as paralogues that were adjacent to each other on the pseudomolecule. All other gene duplications were considered as dispersed, even those in proximity to each other on the same chromosome. The MCScanX parameters were set as follows: Match_score 50, Match_size 5, Gap_penalty -1, overlap_window 5, e_value 1e-05, max gaps = 25. The following genome assemblies were downloaded from Phytozome (<a href="https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/pz/portal.html">https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/pz/portal.html</a>) and analysed: <i>A. thaliana</i> (TAIR10), <i>O. sativa</i> (323_v7.0), and <i>B. distachyon</i> (314_v3.1).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Comparisons with genes annotated in the wheat genomes and other grass genomes</h3><p>To compare the HCC genes of <i>Ae. tauschii</i> with genes annotated in hexaploid bread wheat cv. Chinese Spring (TGACv1 genome assembly and annotation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Clavijo, B. J. et al. An improved assembly and annotation of the allohexaploid wheat genome identifies complete families of agronomic genes and provides genomic evidence for chromosomal translocations. Genome Res. 27, 885–896 (2017)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2941">20</a></sup>) and other grass genomes, three complementary approaches were used: (1) Best BLAST hit analysis, (2) bidirectional best BLAST hit analysis, and (3) OrthoMCL gene family clustering. All analyses used the HCC gene predictions with one representative gene model for each locus. (1) For the best BLAST hit analysis, all <i>Ae. tauschii</i> HCC protein sequences were searched against the TGACv1 wheat HCC gene models (genome-aware) using BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Altschul, S. F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E. W. &amp; Lipman, D. J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403–410 (1990)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2948">69</a></sup> with an <i>E</i>-value cut-off of 10<sup>−5</sup>. Best BLAST hits against TGACv1 wheat gene models were defined by bitscore first, <i>E</i>-value second, and percentage identity third. Results for all <i>Ae. tauschii</i> gene models with hits are reported in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#MOESM1">Supplementary Data 2</a>. (2) For the best BLAST hit analysis, both <i>Ae. tauschii</i> HCC protein and CDS nucleotide sequences were searched against the TGACv1 wheat HCC gene models using BLASTP and BLASTN with an <i>E</i>-value cut-off of 10<sup>−5</sup> and the TGACv1 wheat HCC gene models were used as queries in searches against the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> gene models. (3) Gene family clusters were defined using OrthoMCL version 2.0 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2978">70</a>). Pairwise sequence similarities between all input protein sequences were calculated using BLASTP with an <i>E</i>-value cut-off of 10<sup>−5</sup>. Markov clustering of the resulting similarity matrix was then used to define the orthologue cluster structure, using an inflation value (-I) of 1.5 (OrthoMCL default).</p><p>The following datasets were compared: 39,622 <i>Ae. tauschii</i> HCC gene models, one representative gene model for each locus; 33,032 gene models of sorghum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2993">18</a></sup>; 31,694 gene models of <i>B. distachyon</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="The International Brachypodium Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763–768 (2010)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d49203e2999">25</a></sup>; 39.049 gene models of rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Chantret, N. et al. Molecular basis of evolutionary events that shaped the hardness locus in diploid and polyploid wheat species (Triticum and Aegilops). Plant Cell 17, 1033–1045 (2005)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d49203e3003">71</a></sup>; 39,734 gene models of barley<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Mascher, M. et al. A chromosome conformation capture ordered sequence of the barley genome. Nature 544, 427–433 (2017)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d49203e3007">72</a></sup>; and 104,089 protein sequences of high-confidence gene models of Chinese Spring wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Clavijo, B. J. et al. An improved assembly and annotation of the allohexaploid wheat genome identifies complete families of agronomic genes and provides genomic evidence for chromosomal translocations. Genome Res. 27, 885–896 (2017)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d49203e3012">20</a></sup> allocated to the wheat A, B, and D genomes, one representative gene model for each locus. For wheat, there were 32,452 A-genome gene models, 34,712 B-genome gene models, 32,723 D-genome gene models, and 4,202 unknown-genome gene models.</p><p>The following statistical comparisons were made with the OrthoMCL gene clusters. The numbers of genome-unique clusters in the wheat genomes were compared with the numbers of genome-unique clusters in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome and found to be lower than in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.0001, two-sided test of proportions, <i>n</i> = total numbers of clusters per genome in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). The number of clusters shared by wheat genomes and the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome was compared with the number of clusters shared by <i>Ae. tauschii</i>, barley, <i>B. distachyon</i>, rice, and sorghum, and the former was found to be higher than the latter (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.0001, two-sided test of proportions, <i>n</i> = 15,180, and 12,647). The numbers of B-genome clusters uniquely shared with the D genome and the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome was compared with those uniquely shared by the A genome with the D genome and the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome. The B genome shared more clusters with the D genome and <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome than did the A genome (<i>P</i> = 0.001 and 0.03 for the D genome and <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome, respectively, two-sided test of proportions, <i>n</i> = total numbers of clusters in the A, B, D, and <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genomes) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). Correlation between the number of genome-unique clusters and the number of annotated genes in the genome was also investigated. In the analysis involving the <i>Ae. tauschii</i>, barley, rice, <i>B. distachyon</i>, and sorghum genomes, <i>r</i> = 0.85 (two-tail test, <i>P</i> = 0.06, <i>n</i> = 5) and in the analysis involving the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> and wheat A, B, and D genomes, <i>r</i> = 0.98 (two-tail tests, <i>P</i> = 0.016, <i>n</i> = 4).</p><p>In addition to the BLAST analyses in which targets were gene models, a BLAT analysis was performed using wheat nucleotide sequences as targets<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Clavijo, B. J. et al. An improved assembly and annotation of the allohexaploid wheat genome identifies complete families of agronomic genes and provides genomic evidence for chromosomal translocations. Genome Res. 27, 885–896 (2017)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d49203e3107">20</a></sup>. CDS nucleotide sequences of 39,622 <i>Ae. tauschii</i> HCC genes were used as queries with the BLAT default parameter setting. The best hit of each query was based on the maximum coverage and maximum similarity. Best hits were classified according to the wheat chromosome and genome location of the target sequence.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Gene distribution along pseudomolecules</h3><p>To analyse and graph gene density along the centromere–telomere axis of each <i>Ae. tauschii</i> chromosome, HCC genes in neighbouring 10-Mb intervals, starting from the tip of the short arm in each pseudomolecule, were counted. A sliding window of gene counts averaging three neighbouring 10-Mb intervals was generated. Two of them overlapped between neighbouring sliding windows. One sliding window was started at the tip of the short arm and the other at the tip of the long arm. The two windows moved in the opposite directions. The corresponding means of the two sliding windows were averaged.</p><p>To test homogeneity of the gene density along the chromosomes, we analysed intergenic distances, which have the benefit that gene lengths need not explicitly enter the analysis. A homogeneity assumption on these gene locations corresponds to a homogeneous Poisson process. This has the consequence that intergenic distances should be exponentially distributed. That is, under the assumption of gene distribution homogeneity, the probability density function of intergenic distances is <i>f</i>(<i>x</i>) = <i>λ</i>e<sup><i>−λx</i></sup>, where <i>x</i>, <i>λ</i> &gt; 0 and <i>λ</i> is the rate parameter. In this setup, the rate parameter <i>λ</i> has the interpretation that the expected intergenic distance is 1/<i>λ</i>.</p><p>Tests for the null hypothesis that the intergenic distances follow an exponential distribution were conducted on an ad hoc basis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Gottlieb, A. et al. Insular organization of gene space in grass genomes. PLoS One 8, e54101 (2013)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d49203e3160">27</a></sup> using <i>χ</i><sup>2</sup> goodness-of-fit tests, where some preliminary evidence emerged about the presence of inhomogeneity in gene density. Here we deployed a novel model for intergenic distances using a mixture of exponential distributions, embedding the homogeneous Poisson process into a more general process. A likelihood ratio test was conducted to test the null hypothesis that intergenic distances follow an exponential distribution against the alternative hypothesis that the distances follow a mixture of exponential distributions. This null hypothesis was rejected, indicating that the genes are not uniformly distributed along the chromosomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Prolamin genes</h3><p>Triticeae prolamin gene sequences including high molecular mass glutenin, low molecular mass glutenin, α-, γ-, ω-, and δ-gliadin genes were used in BLASTN queries against Aet v4.0. Matched sequences with an <i>E</i>-value &gt; 10<sup>-10</sup> were extracted and manually annotated to separate full-length, intact genes from pseudogenes. The coordinates of full-length prolamin genes were included into the HCC gene set v2.0.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Disease resistance genes</h3><p>The entire gene set was screened for the presence of RGAs using the RGAugury pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Li, P. C. et al. RGAugury: a pipeline for genome-wide prediction of resistance gene analogs (RGAs) in plants. BMC Genomics 17, 852 (2016)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d49203e3190">23</a></sup>. Four classes of RGAs were analysed: NBS-encoding proteins, receptor-like protein kinases, receptor-like proteins, and transmembrane-coiled-coil proteins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig9">Extended Data Fig. 6b</a>). To compute densities of genes in each RGA class along the pseudomolecules (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig9">Extended Data Fig. 6c</a>) without the confounding effects of higher gene density in distal chromosome regions, a ratio of the RGAs to the total number of genes was computed using a sliding window of 10 Mb and step length of 8 Mb. That meant that there was 2 Mb of overlapping DNA sequence in consecutive windows along the pseudomolecule (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig9">Extended Data Fig. 6c</a>).</p><p>A minimum of three RGAs of the same class that were less than 300 kb apart were arbitrarily considered forming a multi-gene locus. A total of 87 such loci were found and their locations are listed in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 5d</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">Microsatellites</h3><p>The microsatellite identification tool MISA (<a href="http://pgrc.ipk-gatersleben.de/misa/">http://pgrc.ipk-gatersleben.de/misa/</a>) was used to discover microsatellite (SSR) motifs from unmasked and masked pseudomolecules and <i>Ae. tauschii</i> transcripts. A minimum length of 15 bp was used as a limit. A 1-Mb sliding window was used to calculate the density of genes and SSR motifs, and their correlation was calculated using the R statistical computer package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="R: A language and environment for statistical computing, &#xA;http://www.R-project.org/&#xA;&#xA; (2014)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d49203e3227">73</a></sup>. The Seaborn Python package (<a href="https://stanford.edu/~mwaskom/software/seaborn/">https://stanford.edu/~mwaskom/software/seaborn/</a>) was used to produce box plots. A gene density chromosome ideogram was created using the D3js JavaScript library (<a href="https://d3js.org/">https://d3js.org/</a>). SSRs were classified on the basis of their length (≥15 bp and ≥20 bp), motif, and location (repeat-masked, repeat-unmasked, and transcripts) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig11">Extended Data Fig. 8a, c, d</a>).</p><p>We designed a portal for an SSR search in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome sequence (<a href="http://aegilops.wheat.ucdavis.edu/ATGSP/data.php">http://aegilops.wheat.ucdavis.edu/ATGSP/data.php</a>, at the ‘SSR search database’ link). The user has a choice to search for SSRs in genes or in intergenic regions, and to use masked or unmasked pseudomolecules. The output gives the characteristics of the SSR, gives the gene name and pseudomolecule coordinates of the gene in or near which it resides. The user can use the name and look up the gene in the HCC gene colinearity database (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#MOESM1">Supplementary Data 1</a>) or click on the ‘download sequence’ icon and download sequence including the SSR of selected length for primer design or BLAST searches in wheat or <i>Ae. tauschii</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Organellar insertions into the nuclear genome</h3><p>A BLASTN search was conducted with the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> chloroplast genome (GenBank accession, NC_022133) and the <i>T. aestivum</i> mitochondrial genome (GenBank accession, NC_00757) sequences against the unmasked and, later, the repeat-masked <i>Ae. tauschii</i> pseudomolecules. The top hit was recorded. BLAST hits in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> sequence that were located within 200 bp of one another were merged as single hits. BLAST hits that were encompassed by other BLAST hits were removed. The number of insertions per 10 Mb of DNA were counted and the same sliding window approach as described for genes was used to generate graphs depicting the distribution of organellar insertions across each of the pseudomolecules (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig12">Extended Data Fig. 9</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec28">Locations of telomeric sequences</h3><p>Sequences of 42 rice telomeric repeats<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Mizuno, H. et al. Chromosome-specific distribution of nucleotide substitutions in telomeric repeats of rice (Oryza sativa L.). Mol. Biol. Evol. 25, 62–68 (2008)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d49203e3299">9</a></sup> (TTTAGGG) were concatenated, and unmasked pseudomolecules were BLASTN searched for homology to the sequence. The search output was downloaded and the <i>E</i>-value, percentage of identity, and the length of the alignments of detected sequences were recorded. A minimum of five repeats was arbitrarily chosen as a cut-off for considering a hit successful.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec29">Recombination rate and correlation analysis</h3><p>The <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genetic map containing 7,185 SNP markers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Luo, M. C. et al. A 4-gigabase physical map unlocks the structure and evolution of the complex genome of Aegilops tauschii, the wheat D-genome progenitor. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 110, 7940–7945 (2013)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d49203e3317">7</a></sup> was used as an initial database to compute the recombination rate at each HCC gene. To find correspondence between HCC genes annotated in the pseudomolecules and these SNP markers, the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> masked pseudomolecules were searched for homology with the SNP markers using a BLASTN <i>E</i>-value &lt; 10<sup>−10</sup>. Genes that were immediate neighbours of SNP markers but were not hit by the BLAST search, received a cM position of a neighbouring marker that was on the map. These empirical data were treated as realizations from functions along the pseudomolecules. First derivatives were estimated from these functions using local polynomial smoothers; these derivatives allowed for computation of the recombination rate at each gene.</p><p>Pearson’s correlation coefficients of recombination rate and gene density were computed using the average gene density in a 10-Mb interval as one variable and the recombination rate at the midpoint of the 10-Mb interval as the other.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec30">Gene colinearity and structural chromosome analyses</h3><p>Gene colinearity was defined as the shared order of gene starts along two pseudomolecules (one used as a query and the other used as a subject), irrespective of gene orientation. Our arbitrary requirement was that the starts of at least three different genes (five in the study of WGD within the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome and construction of the Circos) were in an ascending or descending order and that the distances between those genes were &lt;0.5 Mb on the subject pseudomolecule (5 Mb on the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> pseudomolecules).</p><p>First, a database was created via a homology search of amino acid sequences of the HCC gene set 2.0 on the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> pseudomolecules (query) and amino acid sequences of all genes in <i>B. distachyon</i>, v3.1, rice, v7.0, and sorghum, v3.1, genome sequences, using BLASTP at an <i>E</i>-value of 10<sup>−5</sup> (subjects). The amino acid sequences of all genes for the four species were downloaded from the Phytozome database. The results were sorted by bit-score in descending order. The top hit for each species was retained as a ‘homologue’ for each <i>Ae. tauschii</i> gene for colinearity analyses and comparisons of chromosome structure.</p><p>Second, a database was constructed from the first database for each <i>Ae. tauschii</i> pseudomolecule with the top hit ordered according to the order of HCC genes along the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> pseudomolecule (query) and corresponding hit of subject gene. If an <i>Ae. tauschii</i> gene was homologous to tandem duplicated genes on the subject pseudomolecule, only one of the duplicated genes was recorded, provided that it was in a colinear position on the pseudomolecule. Subject gene starts showing an ascending or descending order were recorded. To quantify colinearity, the recorded genes were counted and expressed as a percentage of all genes.</p><p>To identify inversions and translocations, the ascending or descending order of genes starts on a subject pseudomolecule was reconstructed by inverting or translocating segments of the pseudomolecules to reconstruct the ancestral colinear gene order. Gene orders in <i>Ae. tauschii</i>, <i>B. distachyon</i>, rice, and sorghum were compared with each other and ancestral and derived orders were inferred based on maximum parsimony. The data were analysed with a paired <i>t</i>-test, using data for individual chromosomes as variables, and Bonferroni correction for multiple comparisons. Smoothed gene-count profiles were generated from the raw counts using local linear smoothers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Müller, H. G. Weighted local regression and kernel methods for nonparametric curve fitting. J. Am. Stat. Assoc. 82, 231–238 (1987)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d49203e3386">74</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Fan, J . &amp; Gijbels, I. Local Polynomial Modelling and its Applications: Monographs on Statistics and Applied Probability 66 (CRC Press, 1996)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d49203e3389">75</a></sup>.</p><p>A database showing colinearity of each of the 38,775 <i>Ae. tauschii</i> HCC genes and those along the <i>B. distachyon</i>, rice, and sorghum pseudomolecules is in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#MOESM1">Supplementary Data 1</a>. Cells containing colinear genes are coloured whereas those that are not colinear are colourless. Changes in gene order due to inversions or translocations are indicated by changes in cell colour. For each structural difference, its start and end on the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> pseudomolecule is indicated. Also indicated for each change is the branch of the grass phylogenetic tree in which the change had taken place and the type of change. Structural changes are coded as follows: A, inversion of 2 genes; B, inversion of 3 genes; C, inversion of &gt;3 genes; D, translocation of 2 genes within a chromosome; E, translocation of 3 genes within a chromosome; F, translocation &gt;3 genes within a chromosome; iT, intercalated translocation between chromosomes; T, terminal translocation; Dup, duplication of a segment; Del, deletion of a segment.</p><p>Inversions and translocations that were discovered in the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome sequence could be assembly errors, particularly inversions involving only two genes. To evaluate this possibility, the presence of 17 randomly selected two-gene inversions in our independently generated PacBio-Illumina hybrid assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Zimin, A. V. et al. Hybrid assembly of the large and highly repetitive genome of Aegilops tauschii, a progenitor of bread wheat, with the MaSuRCA mega-reads algorithm. Genome Res. 27, 787–792 (2017)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d49203e3415">6</a></sup> of <i>Ae. tauschii</i> AL8/78 was determined. All 17 inversions were validated.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec31">Self-synteny within the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome</h3><p>Syntenic blocks within the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome generated by the pan-grass WGD preceding the divergence of grasses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Paterson, A. H., Bowers, J. E. &amp; Chapman, B. A. Ancient polyploidization predating divergence of the cereals, and its consequences for comparative genomics. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 101, 9903–9908 (2004)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d49203e3438">29</a></sup> were identified with the MCScanX package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Wang, Y. P. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d49203e3442">68</a></sup>. The ‘all-versus-all’ BLASTP alignments (<i>E</i>-value &lt; 10<sup>−5</sup>) was performed. The longest protein was used for each of the 38,775 HCC genes. The alignments were analysed with MCScanX using a maximum gap size of 25 and at least five syntenic genes to define duplicate syntenic blocks. Syntenic blocks within the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome were drawn using Circos 0.69 software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Krzywinski, M. et al. Circos: an information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res. 19, 1639–1645 (2009)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d49203e3455">76</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec32">Dot plots</h3><p>The proteins annotated in <i>B. distachyon</i> assembly v3.1, rice v7.0, foxtail millet v2.2, and sorghum v3.1 were downloaded from Phytozome. Only the protein corresponding to the primary transcript was retrieved for each gene. A BLASTP search was conducted of the proteins annotated in <i>Ae. tauschii</i> HCC gene set 2.0 against those retrieved for <i>B. distachyon</i>, rice, foxtail millet, and sorghum. The top two hits with an <i>E</i>-value &lt; 10<sup>−5</sup> were recorded in separate files. The homologous protein pairs were used to detect syntenic blocks using the software MCscanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Wang, Y. P. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)" href="/articles/nature24486#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d49203e3482">68</a></sup> with a match score of 50, gap penalty of −1, <i>E</i>-value of 10<sup>−5</sup>, maximum gap size between any two consecutive protein pairs of 25 and a minimum of five consecutive proteins to declare a syntenic region. This was done separately for the top hits (black) and second-best hits (red) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#Fig13">Extended Data Fig. 10d</a>). The MCScanX output was used to draw comparative dot plots.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec33">Data Availability</h3><p>The RNA-seq reads were deposited in the European Nucleotide Archive as study <a href="http://www.ebi.ac.uk/ena/data/view/PRJEB23317">PRJEB23317</a>. The pseudomolecules plus the unassigned scaffolds have been deposited into GenBank as Aet v4.0 under BioProject <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA341983">PRJNA341983</a>. All new TE families are listed in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature24486#MOESM1">Supplementary Data 4</a>. A JBrowser-based genome browser is available at <a href="http://aegilops.wheat.ucdavis.edu/jbrowse/index.html?data=Aet%2Fdata%2F&amp;loc">http://aegilops.wheat.ucdavis.edu/jbrowse/index.html?data=Aet%2Fdata%2F&amp;loc</a>. BLAST of pseudomolecules and TEs is available at <a href="http://aegilops.wheat.ucdavis.edu/ATGSP/data.php">http://aegilops.wheat.ucdavis.edu/ATGSP/data.php</a>.</p></div></div></section>
